class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div id="mw-indicator-good-star" class="mw-indicator"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Good_articles" title="This is a good article. Follow the link for more information."><img alt="This is a good article. srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/29px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/39px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="185" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Hebrides</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Hebrides_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Hebrides (disambiguation)">Hebrides (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hebridesmap.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Hebridesmap.png/250px-Hebridesmap.png" width="250" height="388" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Hebridesmap.png/375px-Hebridesmap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Hebridesmap.png/500px-Hebridesmap.png 2x" data-file-width="935" data-file-height="1450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hebridesmap.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Inner_Hebrides" title="Inner Hebrides">Inner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>Hebrides</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/i\xcb\x90/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">i\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Gaelic language">Scottish Gaelic</a>: <i lang="gd">Innse Gall</i>, <small>pronounced&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Help:IPA/Scottish Gaelic">[\xcb\x88\xc4\xa9\xcb\x90\xca\x83\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88kaul\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0]</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Norse language">Old Norse</a>: <i lang="non">Su\xc3\xb0reyjar</i>) compose a widespread and diverse <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> off the west coast of mainland <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>. There are two main groups: the <a href="/wiki/Inner_Hebrides" title="Inner Hebrides">Inner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a>. These islands have a long history of occupation dating back to the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>, and the culture of the residents has been affected by the successive influences of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic language">Celtic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Norse language">Norse</a>, and English-speaking peoples. This diversity is reflected in the names given to the islands, which are derived from the languages that have been spoken there in historic and perhaps <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric" class="mw-redirect" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a> times.\n</p><p>The Hebrides are the source of much of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_literature" title="Scottish Gaelic literature">Scottish Gaelic literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_music" title="Gaelic music">Gaelic music</a>. Today the economy of the islands is dependent on <a href="/wiki/Crofting" title="Crofting">crofting</a>, fishing, tourism, the oil industry, and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>. The Hebrides have lower biodiversity than mainland Scotland, but there is a significant presence of seals and seabirds.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="toctext">References and footnotes</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span id="Geology.2C_geography_and_climate"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology,_geography_and_climate">Geology, geography and climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geology, geography and climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg/220px-Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg/330px-Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg/440px-Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Caledonian_MacBrayne" title="Caledonian MacBrayne">Caledonian MacBrayne</a> ferry <a href="/wiki/MV_Hebrides" title="MV Hebrides">MV <i>Hebrides</i></a> leaving <a href="/wiki/Lochmaddy" title="Lochmaddy">Lochmaddy</a> for <a href="/wiki/Skye" title="Skye">Skye</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Inner_Hebrides" title="List of Inner Hebrides">List of Inner Hebrides</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Outer_Hebrides" title="List of Outer Hebrides">List of Outer Hebrides</a></div>\n<p>The Hebrides have a diverse <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> ranging in age from <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> strata that are amongst the oldest rocks in Europe to <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous</a> intrusions.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;Note 1&#93;</a></sup> Raised shore platforms in the Hebrides are identified as <a href="/wiki/Strandflat" title="Strandflat">strandflats</a> formed possibly in <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a> times and later modified by the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary glaciations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The Hebrides can be divided into two main groups, separated from one another by <a href="/wiki/The_Minch" title="The Minch">the Minch</a> to the north and the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_the_Hebrides" title="Sea of the Hebrides">Sea of the Hebrides</a> to the south. The <a href="/wiki/Inner_Hebrides" title="Inner Hebrides">Inner Hebrides</a> lie closer to mainland Scotland and include <a href="/wiki/Islay" title="Islay">Islay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jura,_Scotland" title="Jura, Scotland">Jura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skye" title="Skye">Skye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mull" title="Mull">Mull</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raasay" title="Raasay">Raasay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staffa" title="Staffa">Staffa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Small_Isles" title="Small Isles">Small Isles</a>. There are 36 inhabited islands in this group. The <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a> are a chain of more than 100 islands and small <a href="/wiki/Skerry" title="Skerry">skerries</a> located about 70 kilometres (43&#160;mi) west of mainland Scotland. There are 15 inhabited islands in this archipelago. The main islands include <a href="/wiki/Barra" title="Barra">Barra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benbecula" title="Benbecula">Benbecula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berneray,_North_Uist,_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Berneray, North Uist, Scotland">Berneray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harris,_Outer_Hebrides" class="mw-redirect" title="Harris, Outer Hebrides">Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Lewis" title="Isle of Lewis">Lewis</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Uist" title="North Uist">North Uist</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Uist" title="South Uist">South Uist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/St_Kilda,_Scotland" title="St Kilda, Scotland">St Kilda</a>. In total, the islands have an area of approximately 7,200 square kilometres (2,800&#160;sq&#160;mi) and a population of 44,759.<sup id="cite_ref-GRO_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GRO-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A complication is that there are various descriptions of the scope of the Hebrides. The <i><a href="/wiki/Collins_Encyclopedia_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Collins Encyclopedia of Scotland">Collins Encyclopedia of Scotland</a></i> describes the Inner Hebrides as lying "east of the Minch", which would include any and all offshore islands. There are various islands that lie in the sea lochs such as <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Eilean_B%C3%A0n,_Lochalsh" title="Eilean B\xc3\xa0n, Lochalsh">Eilean B\xc3\xa0n</a></i> and <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Eilean_Donan" title="Eilean Donan">Eilean Donan</a></i> that might not ordinarily be described as "Hebridean", but no formal definitions exist.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the past, the Outer Hebrides were often referred to as the <i>Long Isle</i> (<a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Gaelic language">Scottish Gaelic</a>: <i lang="gd">An t-Eilean Fada</i>). Today, they are also known as the <i>Western Isles</i>, although this phrase can also be used to refer to the Hebrides in general.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;Note 2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Hebrides have a cool temperate climate that is remarkably mild and steady for such a northerly <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>, due to the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>. In the Outer Hebrides the average temperature for the year is 6&#160;\xc2\xb0C (44&#160;\xc2\xb0F) in January and 14&#160;\xc2\xb0C (57&#160;\xc2\xb0F) in summer. The average annual rainfall in Lewis is 1,100 millimetres (43&#160;in) and sunshine hours range from 1,100 \xe2\x80\x93 1,200 <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/per_annum" class="extiw" title="wikt:per annum">per annum</a></i> (13%). The summer days are relatively long, and May to August is the driest period.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Inner_Hebrides" title="Inner Hebrides">Inner Hebrides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Outer_Hebrides" title="History of the Outer Hebrides">History of the Outer Hebrides</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Callanish_standing_stones_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Callanish_standing_stones_1.jpg/220px-Callanish_standing_stones_1.jpg" width="220" height="98" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Callanish_standing_stones_1.jpg/330px-Callanish_standing_stones_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Callanish_standing_stones_1.jpg/440px-Callanish_standing_stones_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1700" data-file-height="757" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Callanish_standing_stones_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Callanish_Stones" title="Callanish Stones">Callanish stone circle</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Hebrides were settled during the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic era</a> around 6500&#160;BC or earlier, after the climatic conditions improved enough to sustain human settlement. Occupation at a site on <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/R%C3%B9m" title="R\xc3\xb9m">R\xc3\xb9m</a></i> is dated to 8590 \xc2\xb195 uncorrected radiocarbon years <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>, which is amongst the oldest evidence of occupation in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> There are many examples of structures from the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period, the finest example being the <a href="/wiki/Callanish_Stones" title="Callanish Stones">standing stones at Callanish</a>, dating to the 3rd millennium BC.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cladh_Hallan" title="Cladh Hallan">Cladh Hallan</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> settlement on South Uist is the only site in the UK where prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Mummy" title="Mummy">mummies</a> have been found.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celtic_era">Celtic era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Celtic era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a></div>\n<p>In 55&#160;BC, the Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> wrote that there was an island called <i><a href="/wiki/Hyperborea" title="Hyperborea">Hyperborea</a></i> (which means "beyond the North Wind"), where a round temple stood from which the moon appeared only a little distance above the earth every 19 years. This may have been a reference to the stone circle at Callanish.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A traveller called Demetrius of Tarsus related to <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> the tale of an expedition to the west coast of Scotland in or shortly before AD 83. He stated it was a gloomy journey amongst uninhabited islands, but he had visited one which was the retreat of holy men. He mentioned neither the <a href="/wiki/Druids" class="mw-redirect" title="Druids">druids</a> nor the name of the island.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first written records of native life begin in the 6th century AD, when the founding of the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a> took place.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This encompassed roughly what is now <a href="/wiki/Argyll_and_Bute" title="Argyll and Bute">Argyll and Bute</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lochaber" title="Lochaber">Lochaber</a> in Scotland and <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">County Antrim</a> in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion1-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The figure of <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Columba</a> looms large in any history of D\xc3\xa1l Riata, and his founding of a monastery on <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> ensured that the kingdom would be of great importance in the spread of Christianity in northern Britain. However, Iona was far from unique. <a href="/wiki/Lismore,_Scotland" title="Lismore, Scotland">Lismore</a> in the territory of the Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn, was sufficiently important for the death of its abbots to be recorded with some frequency and many smaller sites, such as on <a href="/wiki/Eigg" title="Eigg">Eigg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinba" title="Hinba">Hinba</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tiree" title="Tiree">Tiree</a>, are known from the annals.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>North of D\xc3\xa1l Riata, the Inner and Outer Hebrides were nominally under <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> control, although the historical record is sparse. Hunter (2000) states that in relation to King <a href="/wiki/Bridei_I_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridei I of the Picts">Bridei I of the Picts</a> in the sixth century: "As for Shetland, Orkney, Skye and the Western Isles, their inhabitants, most of whom appear to have been Pictish in culture and speech at this time, are likely to have regarded Bridei as a fairly distant presence.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-Hunt44_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt44-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norwegian_control">Norwegian control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Norwegian control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg/220px-Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg.png" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg/330px-Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg/440px-Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1297" data-file-height="1362" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of the Isles">Kingdom of the Isles</a> about the year 1100</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of the Isles">Kingdom of the Isles</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> raids began on Scottish shores towards the end of the 8th century and the Hebrides came under Norse control and settlement during the ensuing decades, especially following the success of <a href="/wiki/Harald_I_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Harald I of Norway">Harald Fairhair</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hafrsfjord" title="Battle of Hafrsfjord">Battle of <i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">Hafrsfjord</i></a> in 872.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In the Western Isles <a href="/wiki/Ketill_Flatnose" title="Ketill Flatnose">Ketill Flatnose</a> may have been the dominant figure of the mid 9th century, by which time he had amassed a substantial island realm and made a variety of alliances with other <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a> leaders. These princelings nominally owed allegiance to the Norwegian crown, although in practice the latter\'s control was fairly limited.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Norse control of the Hebrides was formalised in 1098 when <a href="/wiki/Edgar_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar of Scotland">Edgar of Scotland</a> formally signed the islands over to <a href="/wiki/Magnus_III_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnus III of Norway">Magnus III of Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-H102_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H102-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish acceptance of Magnus III as King of the Isles came after the Norwegian king had conquered <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>, the Hebrides and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> in a swift campaign earlier the same year, directed against the local Norwegian leaders of the various island petty kingdoms. By capturing the islands Magnus imposed a more direct royal control, although at a price. His <a href="/wiki/Skald" title="Skald">skald</a> Bjorn Cripplehand recorded that in Lewis "fire played high in the heaven" as "flame spouted from the houses" and that in the Uists "the king dyed his sword red in blood".<sup id="cite_ref-H102_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H102-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;Note 3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Hebrides were now part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of the Isles">Kingdom of the Isles</a>, whose rulers were themselves vassals of the Kings of Norway. This situation lasted until the partitioning of the Western Isles in 1156, at which time the Outer Hebrides remained under Norwegian control while the Inner Hebrides broke out under <a href="/wiki/Somerled" title="Somerled">Somerled</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Norse-Gael" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse-Gael">Norse-Gael</a> kinsman of the Manx royal house.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the ill-fated <a href="/wiki/Scottish%E2%80%93Norwegian_War" title="Scottish\xe2\x80\x93Norwegian War">1263 expedition</a> of <a href="/wiki/Haakon_IV_of_Norway" title="Haakon IV of Norway">Haakon IV of Norway</a>, the Outer Hebrides and the Isle of Man were yielded to the Kingdom of Scotland as a result of the 1266 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Although their contribution to the islands can still be found in personal and place names, the archaeological record of the Norse period is very limited. The best known find is the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_chessmen" title="Lewis chessmen">Lewis chessmen</a>, which date from the mid 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scottish_control">Scottish control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Scottish control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kisimul_Castle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kisimul_Castle.jpg/220px-Kisimul_Castle.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kisimul_Castle.jpg/330px-Kisimul_Castle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kisimul_Castle.jpg/440px-Kisimul_Castle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kisimul_Castle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kisimul_Castle" title="Kisimul Castle">Kisimul Castle</a>, the ancient seat of <a href="/wiki/Clan_MacNeil" title="Clan MacNeil">Clan MacNeil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castlebay" title="Castlebay">Castlebay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barra" title="Barra">Barra</a></div></div></div>\n<p>As the Norse era drew to a close, the Norse-speaking princes were gradually replaced by Gaelic-speaking <a href="/wiki/Scottish_clan" title="Scottish clan">clan</a> chiefs including the <a href="/wiki/Clan_MacLeod" title="Clan MacLeod">MacLeods</a> of Lewis and Harris, <a href="/wiki/Clan_Donald" title="Clan Donald">Clan Donald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clan_MacNeil" title="Clan MacNeil">MacNeil of Barra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;Note 4&#93;</a></sup> This transition did little to relieve the islands of internecine strife although by the early 14th century the MacDonald <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Isles" title="Lord of the Isles">Lords of the Isles</a>, based on Islay, were in theory these chiefs\' feudal superiors and managed to exert some control.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Lords of the Isles ruled the Inner Hebrides as well as part of the Western Highlands as subjects of the King of Scots until <a href="/wiki/John_of_Islay,_Earl_of_Ross" title="John of Islay, Earl of Ross">John MacDonald</a>, fourth Lord of the Isles, squandered the family\'s powerful position. A rebellion by his nephew, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_of_Lochalsh&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Alexander of Lochalsh (page does not exist)">Alexander of Lochalsh</a> provoked an exasperated <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a> to forfeit the family\'s lands in 1493.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1598, King <a href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI</a> authorised some <a href="/wiki/Fife_Adventurers" class="mw-redirect" title="Fife Adventurers">"Gentleman Adventurers" from Fife</a> to civilise the "most barbarous Isle of Lewis".<sup id="cite_ref-RC12_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RC12-39">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Initially successful, the colonists were driven out by local forces commanded by Murdoch and Neil MacLeod, who based their forces on <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Bearasaigh" title="Bearasaigh">Bearasaigh</a></i> in <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Loch_R%C3%B2g" title="Loch R\xc3\xb2g">Loch R\xc3\xb2g</a></i>. The colonists tried again in 1605 with the same result, but a third attempt in 1607 was more successful and in due course <a href="/wiki/Stornoway" title="Stornoway">Stornoway</a> became a <a href="/wiki/Burgh_of_barony" title="Burgh of barony">Burgh of Barony</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RC12_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RC12-39">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> By this time, Lewis was held by the Mackenzies of <a href="/wiki/Kintail" title="Kintail">Kintail</a> (later the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Seaforth" title="Earl of Seaforth">Earls of Seaforth</a>), who pursued a more enlightened approach, investing in <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a> in particular. The Seaforths\' royalist inclinations led to Lewis becoming garrisoned during the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a> by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Cromwell</a>\'s troops, who destroyed the old castle in Stornoway.<sup id="cite_ref-R42_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R42-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_British_era">Early British era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early British era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clachan_Bridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Clachan_Bridge.jpg/220px-Clachan_Bridge.jpg" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Clachan_Bridge.jpg/330px-Clachan_Bridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Clachan_Bridge.jpg/440px-Clachan_Bridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="585" data-file-height="475" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clachan_Bridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Telford" title="Thomas Telford">Telford</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Clachan_Bridge" title="Clachan Bridge">Clachan Bridge</a> between the mainland of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seil" title="Seil">Seil</a>, also known as the "Bridge across the Atlantic", was built in 1792.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>With the implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> in 1707, the Hebrides became part of the new <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>, but the clans\' loyalties to a distant monarch were not strong. A considerable number of islesmen "came out" in support of the Jacobite <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Mar" title="Earl of Mar">Earl of Mar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_Rising_of_1715" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacobite Rising of 1715">"15"</a> and again in the <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_Rising_of_1745" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacobite Rising of 1745">1745</a> rising including Macleod of <a href="/wiki/Dunvegan" title="Dunvegan">Dunvegan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clan_MacLea" title="Clan MacLea">MacLea</a> of Lismore.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The aftermath of the decisive <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden" title="Battle of Culloden">Battle of Culloden</a>, which effectively ended Jacobite hopes of a Stuart restoration, was widely felt.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>  The British government\'s strategy was to estrange the clan chiefs from their kinsmen and turn their descendants into English-speaking landlords whose main concern was the revenues their estates brought rather than the welfare of those who lived on them.<sup id="cite_ref-H195_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H195-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> This may have brought peace to the islands, but in the following century it came at a terrible price. In the wake of the rebellion, the clan system was broken up and islands of the Hebrides became a series of landed estates.<sup id="cite_ref-H195_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H195-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The early 19th century was a time of improvement and population growth. Roads and quays were built; the <a href="/wiki/Slate" title="Slate">slate</a> industry became a significant employer on <a href="/wiki/Easdale" title="Easdale">Easdale</a> and surrounding islands; and the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Crinan_Canal" title="Crinan Canal">Crinan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caledonian_Canal" title="Caledonian Canal">Caledonian</a> canals and other engineering works such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Telford" title="Thomas Telford">Telford</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/Clachan_Bridge" title="Clachan Bridge">Bridge across the Atlantic</a>" improved transport and access.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> However, in the mid-19th century, the inhabitants of many parts of the Hebrides were devastated by the <a href="/wiki/Highland_Clearances" title="Highland Clearances">Clearances</a>, which destroyed communities throughout the <a href="/wiki/Highlands_and_Islands" title="Highlands and Islands">Highlands and Islands</a> as the human populations were evicted and replaced with sheep farms.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The position was exacerbated by the failure of the islands\' <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a> industry that thrived from the 18th century until the end of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> in 1815<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and large scale emigration became endemic.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Iain_Mac_Fhearchair" title="Iain Mac Fhearchair">Iain Mac Fhearchair</a></i>, a Gaelic poet from <a href="/wiki/South_Uist" title="South Uist">South Uist</a>, wrote for his countrymen who were obliged to leave the Hebrides in the late 18th century, emigration was the only alternative to "sinking into slavery" as the Gaels had been unfairly dispossessed by rapacious landlords.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In the 1880s, the "<a href="/wiki/Camastianavaig" title="Camastianavaig">Battle of the Braes</a>" involved a demonstration against unfair land regulation and eviction, stimulating the calling of the <a href="/wiki/Napier_Commission" title="Napier Commission">Napier Commission</a>. Disturbances continued until the passing of the 1886 <a href="/wiki/Crofters%27_Holdings_(Scotland)_Act_1886" title="Crofters&#39; Holdings (Scotland) Act 1886">Crofters\' Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_economy">Modern economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Modern economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellenabeich_-_geograph.org.uk_-_95384.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ellenabeich_-_geograph.org.uk_-_95384.jpg/220px-Ellenabeich_-_geograph.org.uk_-_95384.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ellenabeich_-_geograph.org.uk_-_95384.jpg/330px-Ellenabeich_-_geograph.org.uk_-_95384.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ellenabeich_-_geograph.org.uk_-_95384.jpg/440px-Ellenabeich_-_geograph.org.uk_-_95384.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellenabeich_-_geograph.org.uk_-_95384.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sea-filled <a href="/wiki/Slate" title="Slate">slate</a> quarries on <a href="/wiki/Seil" title="Seil">Seil</a> (foreground) and <a href="/wiki/Easdale" title="Easdale">Easdale</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Slate_Islands" title="Slate Islands">Slate Islands</a></div></div></div>\n<p>For those who remained, new economic opportunities emerged through the export of cattle, commercial fishing and tourism.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless emigration and military service became the choice of many<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and the archipelago\'s populations continued to dwindle throughout the late 19th century and for much of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Lengthy periods of continuous occupation notwithstanding, many of the smaller islands were abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There were, however, continuing gradual economic improvements, among the most visible of which was the replacement of the traditional thatched <a href="/wiki/Blackhouse" title="Blackhouse">blackhouse</a> with accommodation of a more modern design<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and with the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Highlands_and_Islands_Enterprise" title="Highlands and Islands Enterprise">Highlands and Islands Enterprise</a> many of the islands\' populations have begun to increase after decades of decline.<sup id="cite_ref-GRO_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GRO-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The discovery of substantial deposits of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> in 1965 and the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Scotland" title="Renewable energy in Scotland">renewables sector</a> have contributed to a degree of economic stability in recent decades. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Arnish_yard" class="mw-redirect" title="Arnish yard">Arnish yard</a> has had a chequered history but has been a significant employer in both the oil and renewables industries.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The widespread immigration of mainlanders, particularly non-Gaelic speakers, has been a subject of controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-academia_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-academia-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_the_arts">Media and the arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Media and the arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland-Staffa-Fingals-Cave-1900.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Scotland-Staffa-Fingals-Cave-1900.jpg/220px-Scotland-Staffa-Fingals-Cave-1900.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Scotland-Staffa-Fingals-Cave-1900.jpg/330px-Scotland-Staffa-Fingals-Cave-1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Scotland-Staffa-Fingals-Cave-1900.jpg/440px-Scotland-Staffa-Fingals-Cave-1900.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3557" data-file-height="2641" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland-Staffa-Fingals-Cave-1900.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Entrance to <a href="/wiki/Fingal%27s_Cave" title="Fingal&#39;s Cave">Fingal\'s Cave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staffa" title="Staffa">Staffa</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Many contemporary Gaelic musicians have roots in the Hebrides, including <a href="/wiki/Julie_Fowlis" title="Julie Fowlis">Julie Fowlis</a> (North Uist),<sup id="cite_ref-Thistle_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thistle-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Catherine-Ann_MacPhee" title="Catherine-Ann MacPhee">Catherine-Ann MacPhee</a> (Barra), <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_MacInnes" title="Kathleen MacInnes">Kathleen MacInnes</a> (South Uist), and <a href="/wiki/Ishbel_MacAskill" title="Ishbel MacAskill">Ishbel MacAskill</a> (Lewis). All of these singers have repertoire based on the Hebridean tradition, such as <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Puirt_%C3%A0_beul" title="Puirt \xc3\xa0 beul">puirt \xc3\xa0 beul</a></i></i> and <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">\xc3\xb2rain luaidh</i></i> (<a href="/wiki/Waulking_song" title="Waulking song">waulking songs</a>). This tradition includes many songs composed by little-known or anonymous poets before 1800, such as "<i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Fear_a%27_bh%C3%A0ta" class="mw-redirect" title="Fear a&#39; bh\xc3\xa0ta">Fear a\' bh\xc3\xa0ta</a></i>", "<i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Ailein_duinn" title="Ailein duinn">Ailein duinn</a></i>" and "<i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Alasdair_Mac_Colla#Alasdair_Mac_Colla" title="Alasdair Mac Colla">Alasdair mhic Cholla Ghasda</a></i>".  Several of <a href="/wiki/Runrig" title="Runrig">Runrig</a>\'s songs are inspired by the archipelago; Calum and <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Ruaraidh D\xc3\xb2mhnallach</i> were raised on North Uist<sup id="cite_ref-imdb_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-imdb-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and Donnie Munro on Skye.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Gaelic poet <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Alasdair_mac_Mhaighstir_Alasdair" title="Alasdair mac Mhaighstir Alasdair">Alasdair mac Mhaighstir Alasdair</a></i> spent much of his life in the Hebrides and often referred to them in his poetry, including in <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/w/index.php?title=An_Airce&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="An Airce (page does not exist)">An Airce</a></i></i> and <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birlinn_Chlann_Raghnaill&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Birlinn Chlann Raghnaill (page does not exist)">Birlinn Chlann Raghnaill</a></i></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The best known Gaelic poet of her era, <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">M\xc3\xa0iri Mh\xc3\xb2r nan \xc3\x92ran</i> (<a href="/wiki/Mary_MacPherson" title="Mary MacPherson">Mary MacPherson</a>, 1821\xe2\x80\x9398), embodied the spirit of the land agitation of the 1870s and 1880s. This, and her powerful evocation of the Hebrides\xe2\x80\x94she was from Skye\xe2\x80\x94has made her among the most enduring Gaelic poets.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald2001pp255-7_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald2001pp255-7-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allan_MacDonald_(poet)" title="Allan MacDonald (poet)">Allan MacDonald</a> (1859\xe2\x80\x931905), who spent his adult life on <a href="/wiki/Eriskay" title="Eriskay">Eriskay</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Uist" title="South Uist">South Uist</a>, composed hymns and verse in honour of the Blessed Virgin, the Christ Child, and the Eucharist. In his secular poetry, MacDonald praised the beauty of Eriskay and its people. In his <a href="/wiki/Verse_drama" class="mw-redirect" title="Verse drama">verse drama</a>, <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Parlamaid nan Cailleach</i></i> (<i>The Old Wives\' Parliament</i>), he lampooned the gossiping of his female parishioners and local marriage customs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Murdo_Macfarlane" title="Murdo Macfarlane">Murdo Macfarlane</a> of Lewis wrote <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/C%C3%A0nan_nan_G%C3%A0idheal" title="C\xc3\xa0nan nan G\xc3\xa0idheal">C\xc3\xa0nan nan G\xc3\xa0idheal</a></i></i>, a well-known poem about the Gaelic revival in the Outer Hebrides.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sorley_MacLean" title="Sorley MacLean">Sorley MacLean</a>, the most respected 20th-century Gaelic writer, was born and raised on <a href="/wiki/Raasay" title="Raasay">Raasay</a>, where he set his best known poem, <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Hallaig" title="Hallaig">Hallaig</a></i></i>, about the devastating effect of the <a href="/wiki/Highland_Clearances" title="Highland Clearances">Highland Clearances</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Angus_Peter_Campbell" title="Angus Peter Campbell">Aonghas Ph\xc3\xa0draig Caimbeul</a></i>, described by MacLean as "one of the few really significant living poets in Scotland, writing in any language" (<a href="/wiki/West_Highland_Free_Press" title="West Highland Free Press">West Highland Free Press</a>, October 1992),<sup id="cite_ref-anguspetercampbell_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anguspetercampbell-71">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> and whose Scottish Gaelic-language novel, <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/w/index.php?title=An_Oidhche_Mus_do_She%C3%B2l_Sinn&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="An Oidhche Mus do She\xc3\xb2l Sinn (page does not exist)">An Oidhche Mus do She\xc3\xb2l Sinn</a></i></i>, was voted in the Top Ten of the 100 Best-Ever Books from Scotland, was raised on South Uist.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>The area around <a href="/wiki/Sgurr_Dearg" class="mw-redirect" title="Sgurr Dearg">the Inaccessible Pinnacle of <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Sgurr Dearg</i></a> of <a href="/wiki/Skye" title="Skye">Skye</a> provided the setting for the Scottish Gaelic feature film <i><a href="/wiki/Seachd:_The_Inaccessible_Pinnacle" title="Seachd: The Inaccessible Pinnacle">Seachd: The Inaccessible Pinnacle</a></i> (2006).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The script was written by the actor, novelist, and poet <a href="/wiki/Angus_Peter_Campbell" title="Angus Peter Campbell">Aonghas Ph\xc3\xa0draig Chaimbeul</a>, who also starred in the movie.<sup id="cite_ref-anguspetercampbell_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anguspetercampbell-71">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">An Drochaid</i></i>, an hour-long documentary in Scottish Gaelic, was made for <a href="/wiki/BBC_Alba" title="BBC Alba">BBC Alba</a> documenting the battle to remove tolls from the Skye bridge.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_visitors">Influence on visitors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Influence on visitors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/J.M._Barrie" class="mw-redirect" title="J.M. Barrie">J.M. Barrie</a>\'s <i>Marie Rose</i> contains references to Harris inspired by a holiday visit to <a href="/wiki/Amhuinnsuidhe_Castle" title="Amhuinnsuidhe Castle"><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Amhuinnsuidhe</i> Castle</a> and he wrote a screenplay for the <a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan_(1924_film)" title="Peter Pan (1924 film)">1924 film adaptation</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_and_Wendy" title="Peter and Wendy">Peter Pan</a></i> whilst on <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Eilean_Shona" title="Eilean Shona">Eilean Shona</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hebrides_Overture" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrides Overture">The Hebrides</a></i>, also known as <i>Fingal\'s Cave</i>, is a famous overture composed by <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn" title="Felix Mendelssohn">Felix Mendelssohn</a> while residing on these islands, while <a href="/wiki/Granville_Bantock" title="Granville Bantock">Granville Bantock</a> composed the <i>Hebridean Symphony</i>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Enya" title="Enya">Enya</a>\'s song "Ebud\xc3\xa6" from <i><a href="/wiki/Shepherd_Moons" title="Shepherd Moons">Shepherd Moons</a></i> is named for the Hebrides (see <a href="/wiki/Hebrides#Etymology" title="Hebrides">below</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The 1973 British horror film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wicker_Man_(1973_film)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Wicker Man (1973 film)">The Wicker Man</a></i> is set on the fictional Hebridean island of Summerisle.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The 2011 British romantic comedy <i>The Decoy Bride</i> is set on the fictional Hebrides island of Hegg.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scots_Gaelic_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Scots_Gaelic_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/220px-Scots_Gaelic_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" width="220" height="326" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Scots_Gaelic_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/330px-Scots_Gaelic_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Scots_Gaelic_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/440px-Scots_Gaelic_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 2x" data-file-width="9969" data-file-height="14790" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scots_Gaelic_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geographic distribution of Gaelic speakers in Scotland (2011)</div></div></div>\n<p>The residents of the Hebrides have spoken a variety of different languages during the long period of human occupation.\n</p><p>It is assumed that <a href="/wiki/Pict" class="mw-redirect" title="Pict">Pictish</a> must once have predominated in the northern Inner Hebrides and Outer Hebrides.<sup id="cite_ref-Hunt44_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt44-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> language arrived via <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> due to the growing influence of the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a> from the 6th century AD onwards, and became the dominant language of the southern Hebrides at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Woolf95_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf95-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> For a few centuries, the military might of the <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Norse-Gaels" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse-Gaels">Gall-Gh\xc3\xa0idheil</a></i></i> meant that Old Norse was prevalent in the Hebrides. North of <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Ardnamurchan" title="Ardnamurchan">Ardnamurchan</a></i>, the place names that existed prior to the 9th century have been all but obliterated.<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf95_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf95-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>  The Old Norse name for the Hebrides during the <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> occupation was <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">Su\xc3\xb0reyjar</i></i>, which means "Southern Isles"; in contrast to the <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">Nor\xc3\xb0reyjar</i></i>, or "<a href="/wiki/Northern_Isles" title="Northern Isles">Northern Isles</a>" of Orkney and <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>South of <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Ardnamurchan</i>, Gaelic place names are more common,<sup id="cite_ref-Woolf95_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf95-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and after the 13th century, Gaelic became the main language of the entire Hebridean archipelago. Due to <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> being favoured in government and the educational system, the Hebrides have been in a state of <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a> since at least the 17th century. The <a href="/wiki/Highland_Clearances" title="Highland Clearances">Highland Clearances</a> of the 19th century accelerated the <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">language shift</a> away from Scottish Gaelic, as did increased migration and the continuing lower status of <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic speakers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Local_Studies_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Local_Studies-86">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, as late as the end of the 19th century, there were significant populations of monolingual Gaelic speakers, and the Hebrides still contain the highest percentages of Gaelic speakers in Scotland. This is especially true of the Outer Hebrides, where a slim majority speak the language.<sup id="cite_ref-Local_Studies_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Local_Studies-86">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish Gaelic college, <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Sabhal_M%C3%B2r_Ostaig" title="Sabhal M\xc3\xb2r Ostaig">Sabhal M\xc3\xb2r Ostaig</a></i>, is based on Skye and Islay.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ironically, given the status of the Western Isles as the last Gaelic-speaking stronghold in Scotland, the Gaelic language name for the islands \xe2\x80\x93 <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Innse Gall</i></i> \xe2\x80\x93 means "isles of the foreigners"; from the time when they were under Norse colonisation.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The earliest written references that have survived relating to the islands were made circa 77 AD by <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> in his <i>Natural History</i>, where he states that there are 30 <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Hebudes</i></i>, and makes a separate reference to <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Dumna</i></i>, which <a href="/wiki/William_J._Watson" title="William J. Watson">Watson</a> (1926) concludes is unequivocally the Outer Hebrides. Writing about 80 years later, in 140-150&#160;AD, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, drawing on the earlier naval expeditions of <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Julius_Agricola" title="Gnaeus Julius Agricola">Agricola</a></i>, writes that there are five <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Ebudes</i></i> (possibly meaning the Inner Hebrides) and <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Dumna</i></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Breeze_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Breeze-90">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-91">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-W38_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W38-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Later texts in classical <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>, by writers such as <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Solinus" class="mw-redirect" title="Solinus">Solinus</a></i>, use the forms <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Hebudes</i></i> and <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">H\xc3\xa6budes</i></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The name <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Ebudes</i></i> recorded by Ptolemy may be pre-Celtic.<sup id="cite_ref-W38_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W38-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Islay is Ptolemy\'s <i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Epidion</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-W37_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W37-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> the use of the "p" hinting at a <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Brythonic</a> or Pictish tribal name, <i lang="cel" title="Celtic languages collective text"><a href="/wiki/Epidii" title="Epidii">Epidii</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> although the root is not Gaelic.<sup id="cite_ref-G2487_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G2487-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alex_Woolf" title="Alex Woolf">Woolf</a> (2012) has suggested that <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Ebudes</i></i> may be "an Irish attempt to reproduce the word <i><i lang="cel" title="Celtic languages collective text">Epidii</i></i> phonetically rather than by translating it" and that the tribe\'s name may come from the root <i><i lang="xtg" title="Transalpine Gaulish language text">epos</i></i> meaning "horse".<sup id="cite_ref-woolf_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolf-97">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Watson (1926) also notes the possible relationship between <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Ebudes</i></i> and the ancient Irish <a href="/wiki/Ulaid" title="Ulaid">Ulaid</a> tribal name <i><i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">Ibdaig</i></i> and the personal name of a king <i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">Iubd\xc3\xa1n</i> recorded in the <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Silva Gadelica</i></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-W38_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W38-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The names of other individual islands reflect their complex linguistic history. The majority are Norse or Gaelic but the roots of several other Hebrides may have a pre-Celtic origin.<sup id="cite_ref-W38_92-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W38-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adomn%C3%A1n" title="Adomn\xc3\xa1n">Adomn\xc3\xa1n</a>, the 7th century abbot of Iona, records Colonsay as <i>Colosus</i> and Tiree as <i>Ethica</i>, both of which may be pre-Celtic names.<sup id="cite_ref-W85_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W85-98">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Etymology_of_Skye" title="Etymology of Skye">etymology of Skye</a> is complex and may also include a pre-Celtic root.<sup id="cite_ref-G2487_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G2487-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Lewis is <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">Ljo\xc3\xb0h\xc3\xbas</i></i> in Old Norse and although various suggestions have been made as to a Norse meaning (such as "song house")<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> the name is not of Gaelic origin and the Norse credentials are questionable.<sup id="cite_ref-G2487_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G2487-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest comprehensive written list of Hebridean island names was undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Donald_Monro_(Dean)" class="mw-redirect" title="Donald Monro (Dean)">Donald Monro</a> in 1549, which in some cases also provides the earliest written form of the island name. The derivations of all of the inhabited islands of the Hebrides and some of the larger uninhabited ones are listed below.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Outer Hebrides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Harris" title="Lewis and Harris">Lewis and Harris</a> is the largest island in Scotland and the third largest in the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, after Great Britain and Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> It incorporates Lewis in the north and Harris in the south, both of which are frequently referred to as individual islands, although they are joined by a land border. Remarkably, the island does not have a common name in either English or Gaelic and is referred to as "Lewis and Harris", "Lewis with Harris", "Harris with Lewis" etc. For this reason it is treated as two separate islands below.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The derivation of Lewis may be pre-Celtic (see above) and the origin of Harris is no less problematic. In the <a href="/wiki/Ravenna_Cosmography" title="Ravenna Cosmography">Ravenna Cosmography</a>, <i>Erimon</i> may refer to Harris<sup id="cite_ref-Roman_Map_of_Britain_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roman_Map_of_Britain-102">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> (or possibly the Outer Hebrides as a whole). This word may derive from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">\xe1\xbc\x90\xcf\x81\xe1\xbf\x86\xce\xbc\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">erimos</i> "desert".<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The origin of <a href="/wiki/Uist" title="Uist">Uist</a> (<a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Norse language">Old Norse</a>: <i lang="non">\xc3\x8dvist</i>) is similarly unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-G2487_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G2487-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Island\n</th>\n<th>Derivation\n</th>\n<th>Language\n</th>\n<th>Meaning\n</th>\n<th>Munro (1549)\n</th>\n<th style="width:10%;">Modern Gaelic name\n</th>\n<th>Alternative Greek?\n</td>\n<td>desert\n</td>\n<td>Harrey\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">na Hearadh</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Ptolemy\'s <i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Adru</i>. In <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Norse language">Old Norse</a> (and in <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">modern Icelandic</a>), a <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text"><a href="/wiki/Herred" class="mw-redirect" title="Herred">H\xc3\xa9ra\xc3\xb0</a></i></i> is a type of administrative district.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Alternatives are the Norse <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">haerri</i></i>, meaning "hills" and Gaelic <i><i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">na h-airdibh</i></i> meaning "the heights".<sup id="cite_ref-HS287_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HS287-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Lewis" title="Isle of Lewis">Lewis</a>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Limnu</i>\n</td>\n<td>Pre-Celtic?\n</td>\n<td>marshy\n</td>\n<td>Lewis\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Le\xc3\xb2dhas</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Ptolemy\'s <i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Limnu</i> is literally "marshy". The Norse <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">Ljo\xc3\xb0h\xc3\xbas</i></i> may mean "song house" \xe2\x80\x94 see above.<sup id="cite_ref-G2487_96-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G2487-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/North_Uist" title="North Uist">North Uist</a>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>English/Pre-Celtic?<sup id="cite_ref-G2487_96-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G2487-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Ywst\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Uibhist a Tuath</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>"Uist" may possibly be "corn island"<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> or "west"<sup id="cite_ref-HS287_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HS287-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Scalpay,_Outer_Hebrides" title="Scalpay, Outer Hebrides">Scalpay</a>\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="non" title="Old Uist\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Vatersay" title="Vatersay">Vatersay</a>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Norse\n</td>\n<td>water island<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>Wattersay\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Bhatarsaigh</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>fathers\' island, priest island, glove island, wavy island<sup id="cite_ref-HS287_109-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HS287-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_Hebrides">Inner Hebrides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Inner Hebrides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There are various examples of Inner Hebridean island names that were originally Gaelic but have become completely replaced. For example, Adomn\xc3\xa1n records <i>Sainea</i>, <i>Elena</i>, <i>Ommon</i> and <i>Oideacha</i> in the Inner Hebrides, which names must have passed out of usage in the Norse era and whose locations are not clear.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> One of the complexities is that an island may have had a Celtic name, which was replaced by a similar-sounding Norse name, but then reverted to an essentially Gaelic name with a Norse "\xc3\xb8y" or "ey" ending.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> See for example <a href="/wiki/South_Rona" title="South Rona">Rona</a> below.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Island\n</th>\n<th>Derivation\n</th>\n<th>Language\n</th>\n<th>Meaning\n</th>\n<th>Munro (1549)\n</th>\n<th style="width:10%;">Modern Gaelic name\n</th>\n<th>Alternative Derivations\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Canna,_Scotland" title="Canna, Scotland">Canna</a>\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">Cana</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Gaelic\n</td>\n<td>porpoise island<sup id="cite_ref-HS143_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HS143-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>Kannay\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Eilean Chanaigh</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Possibly from Old Irish <i><i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">cana</i></i>, meaning "wolf-whelp" or Norse <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">kne\xc3\xb8y</i></i> - "knee island"<sup id="cite_ref-HS143_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HS143-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Coll" title="Coll">Coll</a>\n</td>\n<td><i>Colosus</i>\n</td>\n<td>Pre-Celtic\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Colla</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Possibly from Gaelic <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">coll</i></i> - a <a href="/wiki/Hazel" title="Hazel">hazel</a><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Colonsay" title="Colonsay">Colonsay</a>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Pre-Celtic<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Colnansay\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Colbhasa</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Norse for "Columba\'s island"<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-120">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Danna,_Scotland" title="Danna, Scotland">Danna</a></i>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Norse\n</td>\n<td>Unknown<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Danna</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Easdale" title="Easdale">Easdale</a>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Eisdcalfe\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">Eilean Eisdeal</i></i>\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Eas</i></i> is "waterfall" in Gaelic and <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">dale</i></i> is the Norse for "valley".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> However the combination seems inappropriate for this small island. Also known as <i><i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">Ellenabeich</i></i> - "island of the birches"<sup id="cite_ref-HS76_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HS76-123">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Eigg" title="Eigg">Eigg</a>\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">Eag</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Gaelic\n</td>\n<td>a notch<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>Egga\n</td>\n<td><i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Eige</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Also called <i><i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">Eilean Nimban More</i></i> -
